as someone who LOVES to cook but isnt always...the best at it, i got some tips: 1. EGGS! this should be one of the first things you learn to make and master. you'll seriously learn 3/4 of the techniques you need from cooking eggs.  2. read the ENTIRE recipe before you begin...i have screwed up so many recipes because i got cocky only to find out that there's a tricky little step that I didn't read until too late... 3. in addition to the aforementioned knife tips in this video (ps. I love you illymation!) never...ever, put your knife in the sink with the rest of your dishes. I do not have a dishwasher and sliced open my finger reaching into the sink checking for silverware...just carefully scrub your knife with some hot soapy water and put it away. do not put it in the sink

I was the kid that started cooking at 7 years old. By the time I got into high school and started taking cooking classes, I was the kid that impressed everyone around me by knowing how to do all of the techniques already, since I had been doing them for nearly 10 years. In college I attracted my now wife  by making pizza from scratch. And now, with all of that experience and drive to cook  more complicated dishes, I've become the person that everyone among my friends and family get super excited when I cook. A few of my friends even ask me to taste their leftovers of dishes they were proud of, because they want my opinion. They know that I'll give my honest thoughts on it, and they want that so they can become a better cook. And I love that so much. A friend even asked me to take notes at her wedding on all of the food (which I did, and felt really awkward doing while I was there).  All of this to say, I love when people start learning to cook, and I especially love it when they try more difficult dishes that they weren't 100% sure they could pull off.   Last thing. The number one most common critique I give is "don't be afraid to use more salt." Most people under salt their dishes in fear of over salting. The seasoning will be great, but the under salting just leaves the dish with a little something missing. And imo, I prefer a slightly over salted dish than an under salted one.

I've been cooking since my mid-teens; inspired by my mom's cooking. And ever since this summer after moving into a new house with my dad and sister, I started cooking way more. Almost every week. I've cooked chicken and waffles, cheesecake, borsch, brisket, and pasta dishes. It is a lot of fun exploring new dishes and brainstorming ways to plan batches of meals for a low cost. One piece of advice I can give you, Illy, and anyone else reading this (which I should have learned way sooner) is to never overcomplicate things. Last month, I attempted to make those viral potato noodles. The recipe calls for one part potato to one part cornstarch. It took A LOT of work. Boiling the potatoes, mashing the potatoes, mixing the starch, boiling the noodles, and chilling the noodles. Kneading all of that dough was exhausting. And once I tasted the noodles, they were not good. No amount of sauce could obscure that strong taste of cornstarch. Plus, I bought the wrong kind of squash to go with it as the skin made it too tough to eat. So, yeah.

Here's a pro tip from someone whose been cooking since I could reach the countertop, and I'm tall af so that was young.    Never pre-emptively apologize for your food. You think you put too much pepper? Not enough salt? Don't care say nothing unless it's a warning about an allergy. You saying you think it's bad will set the person testing it up to think it's bad. If you say nothing, they'll assume it was intended to taste like that, and appreciate it for what it is, instead of being disappointed because it's not what it could be.

Hi here is a biscuit recipe from my grandma!!  Ingredients  1 1/4 cups of Milk  2 1/2 cups of Self rising flour  1/3 cups of Crisco (or vegetable shortening)   Preheat the oven to 350 degrees F. Sift flour into a large bowl. Add crisco and mix with hands till the mixture looks like sand. Gradually add the milk. Mix the dough until itâ€™s a thick glue texture (chunks are normal). Butter a glass pan and add your dough. It usually makes 5 or 6. Bake in the oven for 40-45 minutes or until the the sides are golden brown. THE NEXT PART IS IMPORTANT. After the biscuits are out. Get a plate (I recommend ceramic plates) cover the pan with the plate grab the sides and flip over pan. If the biscuits donâ€™t fall out the first time. Shake the pan a little. Wait for it to cool and enjoy.

One day I was making cookie bars, And everything was doing okay. I had a friend (Who was good at cooking) with me to help. My friend goes away for a minute or two, and I read the instructions, grabbed a teaspoon, and added some coco powder (Dont remember if it was cocoa, but it was some sort of chocolate powder.)  It looked ok, I felt proud. I read the instructions correctly.. right..? Iâ€™m not a good chef, thatâ€™s one of the reasons why I got my friend to help. So when the cookies were done. Out came a COMPLETELY BROWN BAR. My friend stared at me. And then I realized my mistake. While a tablespoon holds about 15ml, a teaspoon holds 5ml. Therefore, a tablespoon is actually equal to three teaspoons. This is the main difference between tablespoon and teaspoon. I added a TABLESPOON of chocolate powder. Not realizing. So when we were done, we ended up having fudge bars.

When you add too much of one flavoring, you can always get another ingredient to tone down a that flavoring. For example if you add too much pepper, you can always add a little bit of sugar to tone the spice down. Since you added 1/2 of a teaspoon to much pepper, you can add 1/2 a teaspoon of sugar.

I'm Jewish. I have eaten MANY a box matzo ball in from scratch chicken soup. I have nothing bad to say about box matzo balls. They're budget friendly. They're easy. They're quick. And they're satisfying as heck. But when I met my husband's parents, they made my first ever from scratch matzo balls. And..... WOW. They're amazing! Like out of this world amazing. My husband's father spent like 5 years perfecting his recipe. It takes longer and requires executive functioning to plan ahead and let the ingredients chill out overnight instead of just half an hour, it's significantly more expensive, and ditching vegetable oil in favor of duck fat won't give anyone's cardiologist joy, but the result is be most delicious matzo balls I've ever experienced.

Enough russet potatoes (skin or no skin is up to you) to cover the bottom of your pot Half a green cabbage Enough yellow onion for a layer over the taters Salt to taste Tumeric enough to make it green (tumeric turns green when paired with a fat) Butter a WHOLE STICK  So boil the potatoes and when they're soft toss some chopped onions and half the chopped cabbage until that goes soft Next toss in AN ENTIRE STICK OF BUTTER then sprinkle in tumeric until it's highlighter green then salt and pepper to taste ENJOY!

Like Luigi
Since everyone's posting recipes here I'd like to introduce something I figured out how to make a while back: Wonton soup with udon noodles. All it takes is udon noodles, an entire carton of chicken broth, paprika, garlic salt, and frozen mini wontons. What you wanna do is cook the udon noodles first, rinse them when they're done, and put them in your bowl while you make everything else. You dump the carton of chicken broth in the pot and add paprika and garlic salt. How much paprika and garlic salt? I dunno, however much feels right. You heat it on high heat until it's boiling, lower the heat to medium, and then put in a whole bunch of frozen mini wontons, cover the pot, and let it cook for another two minutes. Once it's done, you ladle however much of the soup and wontons you want into the bowl with the noodles. It's cheap, easy, and it slaps. It's also customizable.

Preheat Oven to 450 degrees Fahrenheit (232 degrees Celsius)  Prepare Chicken Thighs or Chicken Legs, or combination thereof. Removing as much surface moisture from chicken, will result in crispier skin. The following recipe does 8 pieces.  Mix together the following spices and seasonings: 2 Teaspoon Onion Powder 2 Teaspoon Garlic Powder 1-1/2 Teaspoon Paprika 1 Teaspoon Thyme 1 Teaspoon Oregano 1 Teaspoon Salt 1/2 Teaspoon black pepper powder  2 Tablespoon olive oil (This is a good starting amount. If you feel more is needed, don't hesitate to add a bit more, 1/2 Tablespoon at a time).  In a bowl coat the chicken with olive oil. You may do it one piece of chicken at a time, or all at once. Be sure to get it under the skin as well. Once coated in olive oil, to your liking, apply Seasoning/spice mix to each piece of chicken, be sure to get it under the skin.  Place coated chicken on a cooking rack, that's on a baking sheet. Bake in oven for 30-40 minutes. Pull out of oven, and let it cool. Then dig in.  If you do not use a cooking rack, and instead place the chicken directly on the baking sheet, you will have to flip the chicken after 15-20 minutes.   Feel free to experiment with spices and seasonings. Example: I once did 3/4 Teaspoon Paprika, and 3/4 Teaspoon Chili Powder; made for spicier chicken.

This video made me so damn hungry... and now I have the urge to just cook something.  I'm not the best, but I love cooking. Especially cooking soup. Soup is magical to me. You fill a pot with water, and just toss in whatever you want. My go-to homemade soup is carrots, potatoes, chicken, green beans, salt, and a hot sauce of my choosing (tends to change, depending on supplies and mood). Also noodles, if I have them. I used to add celery to this mix, but the celery was always stubborn and never softened all the way, and I hate raw celery, so I've since removed it from my list.  I've also learned that if you add some flour to your broth, it thickens it as it cooks. I did that the last time I made soup, and it was awesome.

A quick tip to prevent over-peppering your food: get a set of those tiny glass or silicone bowls and measure each of your seasonings separately and double check before you mix them into the food. Also just a general tip is to take some salt and measuring spoons and measure the salt into your hand repeatedly until you can accurately measure each amount of salt into your hand without the spoons.

PRO TIP, PARENTS: It's okay if you want to spoil your kid by making for them delicious foods (DON'T JUST BUY TAKEOUT!!!) but if you want them to survive in life on good meals, then it's better if you teach them form an early age how to cook. I was just 7 when my mom already instructed me how to make pancakes, muffins and I could do it on my own basically if I knew what to put into the dough. Now, at the age of 20 I am confident in the kitchen and have made a lot of times the dinner and lunch for my family.  Kids are clever and can learn anything safely if they are watched and positively guided through each step. They can adapt to the kitchen well, too.

1:55 I can attest to. I work as a prep cook so I'm chopping stuff a lot. Sharp knifes are less dangerous and less painful because dull knifes have ridges that can catch on objects (that's why it's hard to cut with a dull knife) which means less control. That also means that if you cut yourself, those ridges will catch skin, causing an even worst, even more painful cut. Seriously though, I've had cuts with a stupid sharp blade that didn't hurt.

Omg!!! I'm so happy you talked about matzo ball soup!! It's my FAVORITE soup. I am jewish by blood, we aren't to big on celebrating lots of stuff from the religion but we like Hanukkah and I've been to pass over at my step grandparents house (who are also jewish by blood but also religious). My grandma (my bio mom's mom) would always make matzo ball soup growing up. Granted, it was from a box, and we still make it from a box but it's still SO delicious.   If you haven't tried matzo ball soup I definitely recommend getting a mix and making it. Carrots, onion and celery are what we like to add.  And if you have a big family, I recommend getting an extra box mix because at least for the 3 people in my house eat all the matzo balls and are still hungry. They are pretty airy. Illys explanation is the best I've heard of the soup. XD It's like sponge noodles but I would say taste a bit more eggy then normal noodles. Definitely give it a try of you haven't!!

Recipe for people who are terrible at baking (one of the first things I made on my own):  Ingredients: 1 boxed cake mix, 1 standard can of pumpkin pie filling, and 1 cup of chocolate chips  Steps: Preheat oven to 350 degrees F. Throw the boxed cake mix and pumpkin pie filling in an electric mixer and mix until smooth. Add chocolate chips. Take a mini-muffin tin and spray it with cooking spray. Spoon the batter into the cups, not filling them more than 2/3 full. Here's a quick and easy spaghetti recipe. You'll need: As much spaghetti as you want A pot A spoon or fork As much garlic as you  want A small pan 5-10 cherry tomatoes Olive oil Salt Parmesan cheese  1. Add salt and water to pot. Turn on some ice and let boil 2. Once boiling, add spaghetti. Let spaghetti cook. 3. Set pot aside and peel your garlic. Then crush or chop your garlic into a small pan. Then drizzle with olive oil. 4. Let garlic cook for no more than 1 minute. 5. Now you can strain your spaghetti, and pit on plate. 6. Drizzle on the garlic infused olive oil 7. Cut up 5-10 cherry tomatoes and pit on spaghetti. 8. Put basil on spaghetti. 9. Put parmesan cheese on spaghetti. 10. See if you like it or not.

Quick tips: - In terms of salt, it is better to pour a too little amount of salt than a big amount of salt. - Never, EVER forget to try the food you're making - If you're making something that takes a while, dont sleep. My father had this problem and i remember waking up at 4:30 am because of a smell that made me cough pretty badly, it was something my father wass cooking too late and he slept while he was doing it, so yeah, check to be with enough energy and dont sleep while cooking. - Always check the recipe twice and the ingredients too. Something like this also happened to my father xd (he's a good cook btw). So  my dad was making something like fideua or paella, and he had to add soft pepper (non-spicy pepper) to make it eatable, he confused the soft pepper with the spicy pepper and i was somehow able to eat it xd (it wasnt that bad btw). - Use good cooking material, this is pretty important because if you use pans that when you cook an egg half egg gets sticked to the pan, thats whe you got to buy a new one - Make not so difficult stuff if you're starting, when you're startiong to cook please, do easy stuff like frying an egg or pasta, dont make risotto or something pretty difficult. - Put a timer for *everything* - Put some effort... do i really got to say why? - Dont change anything if you dont know what you're doing, this is important to not ruin anything - Do everything at its time, this goes with the dont change anything if you dont know what you're doing, if you change time to for example how much time you have to cook for it to not be raw and not burnt, just dont change it - Be at what you have to be, dont distract and you will avoid a *d i s a s t e r*   Thats it for the moment, tell me if you got some other tips. Also, if someone was wondering it, im 13... just if i knew too much about cooking xd

As someone who also grew up with matzo ball soup I personally feel that the 2 most important things are the broth and the size of the matzo balls. If the broth is bland then literally everything is bland and it is a bad experience. If the matzo balls are too big IT IS A BAD TIME A VERY BAD TIME  The absolute best matzo ball soup I ever had had tiny matzo balls about 1 inch across with made-from-scratch broth that was just a punch of flavor (in the best, soupy way possible)

Okay, if you have never had matzoball soup, I genuinely feel sorry for you. My family has a specific recipe that's been passed down generations and it is the most delicious thing I've ever tasted. It always makes me feel really at peace and it tastes like home. My Nana makes it on holidays like Passover and Rosha Shonna and Hanukkah. It holds a very special place in my heart, especially considering it's just a soup. It's awesome to hear someone else talk about it in such a fond way because very few people I know have ever had it before. Its super easy, comforting, and cheap ðŸ’œ 1 package of ground beef  1 can of mushrooms  1 can of cream of mushroom soup 1 medium container of sour cream 1 box of pasta  1st, put the raw beef in a skillet and add salt garlic powder paprika and onion powder. Hey, if anyone's curious, homemade milk tea is actually pretty easy! It's not really as good as store bought but eh.  Just get some tea you like, preferably green tea I think. If you use tea leaves and not the packets, use a tea strainer and add a few pinches.  Then pour in boiled water or hot water and let it sit for a bit.  Make sure there's enough room in the cup for milk and stuff.  Add milk to your taste, I like adding cream too. The SUGAR is what makes the milk tea taste good. Add enough of it!!!  If you want to you can also buy some boba, popping pearls can be bought on amazon and you can just scoop it in, tapioca pearls can also be bought on amazon, just microwave them with water till they float to the top, they won't be the best quality in the microwave but stoves scare me. And then pour out the water and pour it in your cup.  In my experience the milk tea looks really pale, I'm not sure how they make it so green or brown. But it tastes great :D There aren't measurements in this, just add stuff little by little till you like it, add some milk and taste it, add some sugar and taste it.

I'm so good at cooking that i can set the house on fire by just stepping in the kitchen
It's great that more "young people" (I'm 31) are getting back to cooking. Cooking is incredibly creative and fun :) here's one of my favorite recipes. It's super easy, cheap, and filling:   You need: pasta of your choice (i use whole grain); 400g cherry tomatos (or more); 2 big bell peppers (any color you like); 2 glasses of a green pesto you like (store bought); 500g of chicken breast OR smoked/fried tofu OR seitan;  1. Cook pasta of your choice. A whole pack (500g).  2. Chop 2 bell peppers into cubes (i prefer red or yellow, but you can also take orange or green, whatever you like). 3. Chop cherry tomatoes into halves or quarters (about 400g or more if you want). 4. Put the veggies and the cooked pasta in a large bowl or pot. 5. Chop the chicken breast into bite-size pieces or prepare the tofu/seitan.  6. Fry the chicken breast or tofu or seitan. Make sure the chicken breast is cooked all the way through! Chicken should NOT be pink on the inside!! (Maybe add a little salt and pepper to whatever you're frying but it's not necessary if you don't like a lot of pepper or salt in your food) 7. Throw chicken/tofu/seitan into the large bowl/pot with everything else.  8. Add the two glasses of pesto and mix well while everything is still hot/warm.  9. DONE.   You will not be able to eat the whole thing but that's okay. Once the bowl/pot is cooled down, you can store the food in the fridge for up to 3 or 4 days. It's also a very easy meal to take with you to work. And it's healthy: it has lean protein, fiber, vitamins from the raw veggies, fats, and carbohydrates for fullness.   You can adapt this base with anything you like, too. I also make this same recipe with pasta as a base, but i put broccoli and chicken in, and I season eveything with olive oil, balsamic vinegar, and salt and pepper. Very light, very nice in the summer :)

Illy, the best advice I can give you is to get a Cast Iron Pan, and just put oil, vegetables and salt, _maybe_ some pepper on it, and throw it in an oven at 400 degrees, usually for 25 minutes, it just works and you don't even need to particularly worry about all the crazy convoluted "what the hell is seasoning? The Cajun?" stuff  Leave that for later, at least your veggies will be handled.  Oh, and have a good pair of oven mitts.  For the blade, a simple whetstone works. Back and forth over each side and up and down the blade and it'll cut like brand new, no more having to figure out of the red juice is coming from your tomatoes or you.

If you want it, this is how I do my â€œfancyâ€ ramen: In a large pot fill and begin boiling. In a small pot, add 2 cups water and begin the boiling process. In the small pot add a big spoonful of miso soup base, then add soba soup base until the color is as dark as you want (I usually do 1/4-1/3 of the bottle) then add fried tofu pieces. Open a can of chicken and rinse twice before adding to the broth pot. Separate the leaves from the stalks of baby bok choy ðŸ¥¬ and add the leaves to the broth pot (you might wanna cut the leaves into smaller pieces but itâ€™s not essential). If the large pot is boiling CarefullY put in an egg (and I also got this adorable egg timer to drop in to make sure itâ€™s done), if the water is already boiling this guarantees that the yolk will be center. If the broth pot is boiling bring the stove down as low as you can while still being on. Cut the stalks of the baby bok choy ðŸ¥¬ into strips, then use a peeler to ribbon a carrot. (Only do this next step with anti cut gloves cause otherwise I always cut my finger nails) Use the peeler to cut a radish or two into thin slices. When the egg is boiled (I recommend a medium-hard boiled if you can), use a spoon to remove from the boiling water, run under cold water to peel. Then cut the egg in half and add to the broth. Using the same pot as the egg, boil the ramen for exactly 3 minutes. When the ramen is cooked, strain and put into a very large bowl. Place the vegetables around the noodles (optional use scissors to top with green onions) then top with the broth and everything in it. Serve hot and sluuuurp.

Bean Casserole sounds great, but if you like baked beans then almost nothing beats Bean Pie. My grandmother used to make it for my father since he was the only vegetarian in the family, and the dish has stuck with him ever since. It's basically just baked beans in olive oil under a layer of steamed then mashed potato topped with cheese. It's baked for about 10 minutes then grilled until the cheese darkens. It's a comfort food that I'll eventually be passing down to my grandchildren too.

WOW this video was well timed!  Yesterday I finally attempted something I had been wanting to make for years, Home-make potstickers. I planned a dinner for my family w/ miso soup, fresh rice, and MANY potstickers. I found a recipe and it was going so well. I spent 2 hours making the dumpling dough, kneading it, rolling it, and using a cookie cutter to cut them out. I carefully stacked them on a plate with flour, and began filling them with the pork-cabbage-green onion mixture. All of the meticulously pre-cut wrappers had melted together into one, big, doughy ball on the plate. I panicked It was already around 6 Pm, I didn;t have time to let the dough rest and roll it out again, and the ball was already rolling on the rice and soup I enlisted the help of my mom and sisters, and cut up the dough ball. We had to stretch each piece of dough by hand, and they were so lumpy and odd. My mom and I devised a new plan for cooking them and we all got to work putting together an assembly line of weird doughy balls and hot oil.   Nothing about these potstickers was going according to my plan, everything was failing, I was literally just wandering around the kitchen in shock for several minutes, I was so upset and frustrated that I had an ADHD meltdown/episode, I coult barely keep from crying and just sitting on the floor and dissociating at the wall. I thought I was at least an intermediate home chef, i thought I would be able to do this, what was wrong with me?  It ended up okay, the cooking method worked and they tasted great, everyone liked them. But I was so exhausted by the end of it (P.S,  Dumpling dough is just as soft and stretchy as it looks)

Another knife safety tip is if you do wind up dropping your knife DO NOT try and grab it before it hits the floor. Everyone has that spilt second reaction to try and grab things before they hit the floor and it might take a while to train your brain, but please don't try and catch it. Just back up so it can't hit your leg or foot and then when it hits the floor you can pick it up, rinse it off and continue working.

Here's a recipe for good scrambled eggs. Two eggs Dash of salt Crackering of black pepper Smacking of dried garlic Italian seasoning (add as much as you want, my lil sis once added wayy too much and it was delicious)  Combine & mix all in bowl, and pour in BUTTERED pan. Now, the secret for adjustable scrambling size is how long you keep it on the pan before you scramble it. Longer means bigger! Once it is no longer gooey, you can serve it.   Bone apple tea.

Cooking tip for people who are either too busy or overwhelmed by cooking: invest in a big crockpot. It's super easy to cook in because, depending on what you decide to make, you pretty much just dump your ingredients in it in the morning and in 6-8 hours it's done. Another easy soup to make is beef barley soup. I use 4 cups beef bone broth, 3 or 4 cans of Campbell's beef consomme, one pound of ground beef or roughly chopped steak, a bunch of mushrooms, carrots, celery, onion, and spinach plus 1/4 to 1/2 cup of quick barley. And add salt and pepper or any seasoning to taste. Just don't make the mistake I made my first time.. adding the whole box to my soup, severely underestimating the absorbative powers of barley. Even with a smaller amount it soaks up all of the broth when you put it away. (It comes back when reheated or you can add another can of consomme)

She's right about the ' a sharp knife is less dangerous than a dull one' I was in a cooking class, and do have ServSafe Management certification. If you put your thumb, and index finger on each side of the knife spine, just above the handle, it can help with grip so it won't flop around a lot. You are supposed to use the heel of a knife, unless a serrated or a boning knife (which uses only the tip)

I needed this video! I made "Salt and Pepper" shrimp last night.  I watched like 4 videos on how to do it. I was feeling good, confident even.  So, instead of breading the shrimp in corn starch...I used BAKING SODA.  It didn't look right, it didn't feel right, it really didn't smell right.  When I put them in the oil it didn't seem right.  It didn't brown and the shrimp sorta shriveled up.  I finished the all the shrimp, $20 worth of shrimp, and laid them out on paper towels.  That's when it hit me.  But...maybe it wouldn't be too bad right, baking soda is in cake, I like cake.  Oh my god no! No! Turns out baking soda is really salty and acidic and sour and angry and chemically and does not improve with frying.  It was like I fried shrimp in salt and hair spray. One of the easiest recipes my mom taught me is carne y patatas I'd love for you to try it it's nice comfort food  You take a pound of ground beef and brown it in a large cooking pot   Then you put in your potatoes they should be sliced into cubes you only need two large potatoes  Then add a small can of tomato sauce I like to use the tomato basil garlic kind   Pour in water until your meat and potatoes are completely covered  Then add cumin powder garlic powder salt and pepper to taste   Let it sit covered for 45 minutes and then it's ready to serve    Hope you try it and enjoy it

Hey Illy, if you don't know what to cook, then try Hello Fresh or Blue Apron, they send you recipes and ingredients weekly and it's a lot cheaper than going on a grocery run. You can choose what kind of meal options you want and what meals you want to cook. My personal favorites are probably Hello Fresh's "Italian Chicken and Pepper Sandos with Potato Wedges" and the "Velvety Mushroom Prosciutto Chicken with Scallion Mashed Potatoes & Roasted Broccoli" (I take out the mushrooms because that's just me). The meals are pretty good, they don't take very long to make, and they are cheap. 4 meals a week is $80, that's cheaper than most grocery store runs.

Honestly, best tips I can give for anyone who's learning to cook: find recipes, follow the instructions, don't try to 'wing it' till you get a good grip on cooking. I started out with a discount on hello fresh boxes, I don't know if they still do this but I got to see the difficulty as I picked what they were going to deliver and worked my way up.  >knifes!? Cut away from your hands! Peelers?! Move away from your hands! Anything sharp? Move away from your haaaaaandssssssss  >If you frequently burn things in the oven, start checking on it every 10 minutes after half of the indicated time has passed. _Always_ double check the temperature.  >If you frequently burn things in the pan: sometimes food is a prick. You're watching the pasta water not do anything, walk away to grab something and boom- it boils over. Nasty stuff, you can only counter that by sticking around. If it's too boring, maybe listen to some music. That way you can still keep your eyes on the food.

I've found precooked bags of chicken near the deli of my store & they're a Godsend! I use them with chicken & rice soup with a can & 1/2 of water, a shot of hot sauce (Might be too much for normal people) & throw some cheese on top. They have different flavors too like rotisserie, fajita, grilled, roasted, all of which can give different flavors. I also like using the family sized bags for curry, don't need to spend all day prepping. I use plain diced frozen breakfast potatoes & frozen cut carrots, just boil em & toss em in the roux while it's dissolving in water along with the chicken & prep some rice. Usually only takes about half an hour.
